<div id="review_130483244" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/130483244" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/130483244?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780440841210" href="/book/show/2842.Bridge_to_Terabithia">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      Jzhunagev, Joyzi
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=ya">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer130483244&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer130483244" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10245421982331857824">If Thornton Wilder's <i>The Bridge of San Luis Rey</i> (1927) is about a bridge that fell down and killed 5 people, Katherine Paterson's <i>Bridge to Terabithia</i> (1977) is about a bridge that is put up <u>because</u> of <i>a</i> person's death.<br/><br/>In this children's book, American novelist Katherine Paterson (born 1932) created a make believe world of <b>Terabithia</b> whose name she unconsciously coined from C. S. Lewis' <i>The Voyage of the Dawn Treader</i> where Lewis has an island called <i>Terebinthia</i>. However she added that Lewis prob</span>
  <span id="freeText10245421982331857824" style="display:none">If Thornton Wilder's <i>The Bridge of San Luis Rey</i> (1927) is about a bridge that fell down and killed 5 people, Katherine Paterson's <i>Bridge to Terabithia</i> (1977) is about a bridge that is put up <u>because</u> of <i>a</i> person's death.<br /><br />In this children's book, American novelist Katherine Paterson (born 1932) created a make believe world of <b>Terabithia</b> whose name she unconsciously coined from C. S. Lewis' <i>The Voyage of the Dawn Treader</i> where Lewis has an island called <i>Terebinthia</i>. However she added that Lewis probably based his island's name from <i>Terebinth</i> tree in the Holy Bible so they both <i>pinched from somewhere else, probably unconsciously</i>.<br /><br />Anyways, enough about the name. It's just that I was asked twice already about the meaning of the title of the book that I reviewed so it is becoming part of my psyche to always ask myself the meaning of the title after reading the book especially if the reason is not clear or obvious.<br /><br />The story, set in a small town in the US, is about two lonely 10 year olds, <b>Jesse Aarons</b> and <b>Leslie Burke</b> who find each other's company enjoyable because they are different from the rest of the grade schoolers. Jesse is fond of drawing and he feels unappreciated. Leslie comes from the city, reads a lot of children's fantasy books (her parents are writers) and does not have TV set at home. Before Leslie transfers to Jesse's school, he (Jesse) is the fastest runner. So, their friendship starts with jealousy (because Leslie runs faster than Jesse) and hate but they end up as king and queen of Leslie's make believe world of Terabithia.<br /><br />Sweet story. I am not surprised that even young adults in their teens, 20's and even 30's find this book amazing and rate it with 5 stars. It brings back memories of make-believe worlds when we were young. Especially for those who are in their early 20's and starting their careers, <i>welcome to the real world, dudes!</i>. No more allowances from parents and you have to scrimp yourselves with what you earn. Since you are starting, your salary is ,eager but you are ashamed to ask mom or dad anymore. You want to prove your independence. For those who are starting their families, more budgeting skills are needed. These hardships in real world are sometimes reasons enough for you to think of retreating back to your former secured happy worlds of make-believes. So now, even for few hours, you want to go back there: when you were young and not worrying about money or relationships. Like being in Terabithia and you can do whatever you want because you are the King like Aaron or the Queen like Leslie. Sometimes, you even want to cry <i>oh, I hated the book for making me cry especially when Leslie died</i> because of the pain of realization. You are no longer a child. You are now too old for make-believes. But hey, that's life. You will grow old too. We will all die.<br /><br />Am I not right? </span>
  <a data-text-id="10245421982331857824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15762417680644543373" style="display:none">When I read this in 5th grade, I really liked it for the first 75% of the story. WHAT?" I refused to believe that anyone could DIE just by falling into a creek, and still think it's a really stupid way to kill off a character. <br /><br />Read for: 5th grade English</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780439366779" href="/book/show/2839.Bridge_to_Terabithia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1424859-bonnie?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1424859-bonnie?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1424859-bonnie?shelf=recommended">recommended</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer54973859" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3091817859746639335">Janusz Korczak Medal (Poland) 1981<br/>Silver Pencil Award (Netherlands) 1981 <br/>Newbery Medal 1978 <br/>ALA Notable Children's Books 1977<br/>School Library Journal Best Book of 1977<br/>Lewis Carroll Shelf Award, 1978<br/>Le Grand Prix des Jeunes Lecturs (France), 1986<br/>1986 Colorado Blue Spruce Young Adult Book Award<br/><br/>Note also that on the American Library Association Reading List: Bridge to Terabithia (along with Tuck Everlasting) is one of six books recommended for 9-12 year-old children. This is especially heartening sin</span>
  <span id="freeText3091817859746639335" style="display:none">Janusz Korczak Medal (Poland) 1981<br />Silver Pencil Award (Netherlands) 1981 <br />Newbery Medal 1978 <br />ALA Notable Children's Books 1977<br />School Library Journal Best Book of 1977<br />Lewis Carroll Shelf Award, 1978<br />Le Grand Prix des Jeunes Lecturs (France), 1986<br />1986 Colorado Blue Spruce Young Adult Book Award<br /><br />Note also that on the American Library Association Reading List: Bridge to Terabithia (along with Tuck Everlasting) is one of six books recommended for 9-12 year-old children. This is especially heartening since “the novel's content has been the frequent target of censors and appears at number nine on The American Library Association list of the 100 Most Frequently Challenged Books for the decade 1990–2000.” (Wikipedia)Apparently, the banning, which occurred after year 2000 as well, was for offensive language, sexual content and Occult/Satanism. <br /><br />There are a few mild swear words in the book, and they are merely used for emphasis. If having a crush on a girl means the book contains sexual content, and if creating a magic kingdom (Terabithia) in which to play at being king and queen means dabbling in the occult or Satanism, well… need I say more?<br /><br />Death and what happens to a person after dying are also discussed in the book. Perhaps some adults think children shouldn’t read about death. Personally, I commend Patterson for writing a moving story for children that addresses adult themes, and yet is never condescending. <br /><br />I read this book as an adult, wishing I had read it when I was younger. Of course, I may have cried harder then. Highly recommended for readers from ages 9 and up – all the way up – to adulthood and through old age!<br /></span>

<div id="review_2800308448" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2800308448" />
    <a title="Lauren Lanz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82987361-lauren-lanz"><img alt="Lauren Lanz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586150400p2/82987361.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2800308448?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren Lanz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren Lanz" href="/user/show/82987361-lauren-lanz">Lauren Lanz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82987361-lauren-lanz?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82987361-lauren-lanz?shelf=author-of-colour">author-of-colour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82987361-lauren-lanz?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82987361-lauren-lanz?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82987361-lauren-lanz?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2800308448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15648266343626127587">I first took a liking to this book because Jesse is a runner. He reminded me of my younger self, as I was always proud to run cross country and perform as starter for my school’s relay racing team. His passion resonated with me deeply, which was lovely.<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Sometimes it seemed to him that his life was delicate as a dandelion. One little puff from any direction, and it was blown to bits.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/><i>Bridge to Terabithia</i> follows a friendship between a young boy and girl, Jesse and Leslie. The two beco</span>
  <span id="freeText15648266343626127587" style="display:none">I first took a liking to this book because Jesse is a runner. He reminded me of my younger self, as I was always proud to run cross country and perform as starter for my school’s relay racing team. His passion resonated with me deeply, which was lovely.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Sometimes it seemed to him that his life was delicate as a dandelion. One little puff from any direction, and it was blown to bits.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br /><i>Bridge to Terabithia</i> follows a friendship between a young boy and girl, Jesse and Leslie. The two become acquaintances almost immediately upon Leslie’s move into the town. Together they create an imaginary kingdom in the woods called Terabithia, as their way of escaping reality.<br /><br />Jesse was easily my favourite thing about this book. I enjoyed hearing about his love for running and art of any kind. It also made me happy to see Katherine Paterson comment on gender roles through her characters, making it so that they didn’t have to conform to anyone’s expectations.<br /><br />I found much of this story quite uneventful, though perhaps it’s meant to be that way due to central themes of simple friendship and grief. Regardless, I wished for something more from this book. I know this is a tale that broke the hearts of many, but I felt there wasn’t enough time to truly understand the characters before tragedy struck. Considering this is a book largely based on teaching about hurt and healing, I was surprised at how the ending was glossed over, leaving Jesse with little to no closure.<br /><br />I definitely enjoyed Katherine Patterson’s writing, and can easily see why <i>Bridge to Terabithia</i> resonates with people of all ages!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15648266343626127587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer235597466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17047145858555436947"><br/>For the record I am not an outwardly emotional person. Okay, let me get that right. I can be a bubbly energetic or excited individual from time to time. Of course, that isn't what I meant. I'm quite a content, optimistic kind of person so I am emotionally driven - very much so. What I mean to say is that I thrive in my life as a laconic, down to earth kind of person. I'm laid back and when it comes to outward expressions of emotion I tend to internalise. I would still consider myself an extrover</span>
  <span id="freeText17047145858555436947" style="display:none"><br />For the record I am not an outwardly emotional person. Okay, let me get that right. I can be a bubbly energetic or excited individual from time to time. Of course, that isn't what I meant. I'm quite a content, optimistic kind of person so I am emotionally driven - very much so. What I mean to say is that I thrive in my life as a laconic, down to earth kind of person. I'm laid back and when it comes to outward expressions of emotion I tend to internalise. I would still consider myself an extroverted introvert it's just that it is very hard to visibly stun me, shock me or make me cry. I'm not insensitive. I just don't show my reactions most of the time.<br /><br />So, why did I just spend countless sentences on my emotional personality? Mainly to try and convey the fact that this novel right here is one of the few to have rocked me emotionally. It made me raw at the end and it made me tear up. The film has a similar impact. In fact the film is a fairly strong adaptation of this sad, beautiful teardrop of a book.<br /><br />Let me head off on another tangent. I love conclusions. Conclusions are almost my favourite part of any story, save for the fact that it means the book is over and you're left back in reality. However, for this book the conclusion was terrible, shocking, unacceptable. It was too emotionally moving. The happy ending I was expecting wasn't there. And that is why it is a brilliant book: trust me, read it and see if it doesn't touch you in some way. My one warning is that it is a children's novel of course.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17047145858555436947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hqKtTluQz1CjAj2HoaoSo8wSjixOkynas+8Y8wf91L28hMSc4M4GOAQpieZWXxh8Gk1p5IAep73okV5Cyr4mJw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

There is no lake at Camp Green Lake. But there are an awful lot of holes.\n\nIt doesn’t take long for Stanley to realize there’s more than character improvement going on at Camp Green Lake.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1949.Katherine_Paterson">About Katherine Paterson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1949.Katherine_Paterson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1595481183p3/1949.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1949.Katherine_Paterson">Katherine Paterson
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16865169788621608872">From author's website:<br/><br/>People are always asking me questions I don't have answers for. One is, "When did you first know that you wanted to become a writer?" The fact is that I never wanted to be a writer, at least not when I was a child, or even a young woman. Today I want very much to be a writer. But when I was ten, I wanted to be either a movie star or a missionary. When I was twenty, I wanted t</span>
  <span id="freeText16865169788621608872" style="display:none">From author's website:<br /><br />People are always asking me questions I don't have answers for. One is, "When did you first know that you wanted to become a writer?" The fact is that I never wanted to be a writer, at least not when I was a child, or even a young woman. Today I want very much to be a writer. But when I was ten, I wanted to be either a movie star or a missionary. When I was twenty, I wanted to get married and have lots of children.<br /><br />Another question I can't answer is, "When did you begin writing?" I can't remember. I know I began reading when I was four or five, because I couldn't stand not being able to. I must have tried writing soon afterward. Fortunately, very few samples of my early writing survived the eighteen moves I made before I was eighteen years old. I say fortunately, because the samples that did manage to survive are terrible, with the single exception of a rather nice letter I wrote to my father when I was seven. We were living in Shanghai, and my father was working in our old home territory, which at the time was across various battle lines. I missed him very much, and in telling him so, I managed a piece of writing I am not ashamed of to this day.<br /><br /><br />A lot has happened to me since I wrote that letter. The following year, we had to refugee a second time because war between Japan and the United States seemed inevitable. During World War II, we lived in Virginia and North Carolina, and when our family's return to China was indefinitely postponed, we moved to various towns in North Carolina, Virginia, and West Virginia, before my parents settled in Winchester, Virginia.<br /><br />By that time, I was ready to begin college. I spent four years at King College in Bristol, Tennessee, doing what I loved best-reading English and American literature-and avoiding math whenever possible.<br /><br />My dream of becoming a movie star never came true, but I did a lot of acting all through school, and the first writing for which I got any applause consisted of plays I wrote for my sixth-grade friends to act out.<br /><br />On the way to becoming a missionary, I spent a year teaching in a rural school in northern Virginia, where almost all my children were like Jesse Aarons. I'll never forget that wonderful class. A teacher I once met at a meeting in Virginia told me that when she read Bridge to Terabithia to her class, one of the girls told her that her mother had been in that Lovettsville sixth grade. I am very happy that those children, now grown up with children of their own, know about the book. I hope they can tell by reading it how much they meant to me.<br /><br /><br />After Lovettsville, I spent two years in graduate school in Richmond, Virginia, studying Bible and Christian education; then I went to Japan. My childhood dream was, of course, to be a missionary to China and eat Chinese food three times a day. But China was closed to Americans in 1957, and a Japanese friend urged me to go to Japan instead. I remembered the Japanese as the enemy. They were the ones who dropped the bombs and then occupied the towns where I had lived as a child. I was afraid of the Japanese, and so I hated them. But my friend persuaded me to put aside those childish feelings and give myself a chance to view the Japanese in a new way.<br /><br />If you've read my early books, you must know that I came to love Japan and feel very much at home there. I went to language school, and lived and worked in that country for four years. I had every intention of spending the rest of my life among the Japanese. But when I returned to the States for a year of study in New York, I met a young Presbyterian pastor who changed the direction of my life once again. We were married in 1962.<br /><br />I suppose my life as a writer really began in 1964. The Presbyterian church asked me to write some curriculum materials for fifth- and sixth-graders. Since the church had given me a scholarship to study and I had married instead of going back to work in Japan, I felt I owed them something for their m</span>
  <a data-text-id="16865169788621608872" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
